    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer9434742754095331011">The Five find adventure, when they spend Easter vacation at Mr. Lenoir's sinister house Smuggler's Top. Set high above an eerie marsh, the house is honeycombed with hidden staircases and tunnels that once served as a hideaway for smugglers. When strange lights begin to appear, the Five suspect that the tunnels are once more in use.</span>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_211652514" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/211652514" />
    <a title="Mark Lawrence" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4508542-mark-lawrence"><img alt="Mark Lawrence" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442536170p2/4508542.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/211652514?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mark Lawrence" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mark Lawrence" href="/user/show/4508542-mark-lawrence">Mark Lawrence</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer211652514" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13469330727968139521">In this one the children are evicted from Kirrin Cottage (3 of them just visiting anyway) by a falling tree and Uncle Quentin packs them off to stay with a fellow scientist who (because in Blyton's imagination all scientists are rich (with the sole exception of Quentin himself in book 1 ... until all the gold) lives in a huge house riddled with secret tunnels that lead into a wide cave system.) <br/><br/>Caves and tunnels are a staple of this series. Few, if any, of the 23 books don't go underground or th</span>
  <span id="freeText13469330727968139521" style="display:none">In this one the children are evicted from Kirrin Cottage (3 of them just visiting anyway) by a falling tree and Uncle Quentin packs them off to stay with a fellow scientist who (because in Blyton's imagination all scientists are rich (with the sole exception of Quentin himself in book 1 ... until all the gold) lives in a huge house riddled with secret tunnels that lead into a wide cave system.) <br /><br />Caves and tunnels are a staple of this series. Few, if any, of the 23 books don't go underground or through the walls at some point.<br /><br />Now Uncle Quentin, who in book one was writing formulas in his secret books and brewing stuff in test tubes, seems to have morphed into a civil engineer and is collaborating with their new host, Mr Lenoir, to drain the swamp, in a literal rather than Trumpian, sense.<br /><br />The original conflict is over the taking of Timmy. Mr Lenoir hates dogs. This is generally an unfailing indicator of villainy!<br /><br />The wider conflict concerns the local smuggler who uses the marshes, and rather unbelievably ends up kidnapping Uncle Quentin in some implausible plan to thwart the draining by buying then burning his plans, thus stopping ... the swamp ... from being drained? Also, the smuggler is very rich and only smuggles for fun.<br /><br />The central lesson of this book is that if you see a light out in the dark, then someone is up to no good. Generally smuggling.<br /><br />The conflict that I recall strongly from my reading as a child was between the Five and the deaf manservant Block who they suspect might be able to hear, and who is out to expose Timmy who has been hiding in the house's secret passages in order that Mr Lenoir not know he was in the house.<br /><br />Not one of my favourites in the series. A bit insipid.<br /><br />As a footnote: Enid seems to have a fascination with deafness. Block pretends / or doesn't to be deaf in this book. A girl in Malory Towers fakes deafness to general hilarity. The mainstay of the humour in the Faraway Trilogy is the Saucepan Man's hardness of hearing and failure to understand.<br /><br /> <a href="http://eepurl.com/cimnK1" rel="nofollow noopener">Join my 3-emails-a-year newsletter #prizes</a><br /><br /><br />..</span>
  <a data-text-id="13469330727968139521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1500713012&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1500713012" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"

<span id="freeTextContainer4226631576123729196">This entry in the Famous Five series is really quite dark. The book starts with the top floor of George's house being completely destroyed by storm damage. George's parents, feeling that the house is a dangerous place for George and the rest of the gang (who were staying with George at the time of the storm) to be around (fair enough) decide to send the kids to stay with <i>a complete stranger</i> who George's father has received a letter from concerning his scientific research. This guy turns out to b</span>
  <span id="freeText4226631576123729196" style="display:none">This entry in the Famous Five series is really quite dark. The book starts with the top floor of George's house being completely destroyed by storm damage. George's parents, feeling that the house is a dangerous place for George and the rest of the gang (who were staying with George at the time of the storm) to be around (fair enough) decide to send the kids to stay with <i>a complete stranger</i> who George's father has received a letter from concerning his scientific research. This guy turns out to be a drooling psychopath who swiftly murders the four children and their little dog too!<br /><br />...<br /><br />OK, not really... BUT HE COULD HAVE BEEN is my point. It turns out that this chap is extremely grumpy and lives at the top of an island (which I'm pretty sure is a thinly disguised St. Michael's Mount, complete with a causeway and everything) that turns out to be honeycombed with secret smugglers' tunnels. Guess who's still using the smugglers' tunnels? That's right: smugglers. <i>Who could have possibly seen that coming?!</i><br /><br />Despite the nonsensical nature of some of it, I did actually really enjoy this book. It's very exciting and will surely go down very well with children and those who are still children at heart. It's full of moments that will make you go 'What?! No!!! Surely not...'<br /><br />P.S. - Bizarrely, this is the <i>only</i> book in the Famous Five series (which is twenty two books long) that doesn't have an unabridged version available on Audible. The book is on Audible, but only in the dreaded abridged format. I mean, seriously, who wants to read an abridged version of a book? I'm kind of disgusted that abridging books is even a thing, to be honest.<br /><br /><i>Buddy read with Sunshine Seaspray</i></span>

          </span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340681091" href="/book/show/862891.Five_Go_to_Smuggler_s_Top">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3224577460" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7515530772324198354">Wow, I can’t believe it took us a month to read this book. If Dante had been in school we wouldn’t have watched TV or movies so late in the evening and run out of time for bedtime reading. So, I switched to a new tactic: reading to him at breakfast and/or lunch. <br/><br/>Anyway, after only having read the first one of the series, I pretty much twigged to her formula: slow start, setting the scene, bring in an element of mystery, then wham! Pick up the pace, add some peril for one or more of the kids and </span>
  <span id="freeText7515530772324198354" style="display:none">Wow, I can’t believe it took us a month to read this book. If Dante had been in school we wouldn’t have watched TV or movies so late in the evening and run out of time for bedtime reading. So, I switched to a new tactic: reading to him at breakfast and/or lunch. <br /><br />Anyway, after only having read the first one of the series, I pretty much twigged to her formula: slow start, setting the scene, bring in an element of mystery, then wham! Pick up the pace, add some peril for one or more of the kids and the dog’s the hero! <br /><br />Still, they are highly entertaining and useful cultural history lesson (of a certain set of people). It’s weird reading them out loud with my American accent (but my son won’t even let me try to affect a British one), especially when they use British slang or manner of speaking. But I’m entertained by the dichotomy, even if my son doesn’t notice. <br /><br />At any rate, Smuggler’s Top in Castaway sounds like a really fascinating place. Too bad it’s (most likely) completely made up. I mean, a house on the top of a hill, with towers and secret passageways, surrounded by marshes, next to the sea? I’d love to go on a tour. </span>

<div id="review_1953162" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1953162" />
    <a title="Namratha" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/114559-namratha"><img alt="Namratha" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1406702134p2/114559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1953162?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Namratha" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Namratha" href="/user/show/114559-namratha">Namratha</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340040003" href="/book/show/2205945.Five_Go_to_Smuggler_s_Top">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/114559-namratha?shelf=children-s">children-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/114559-namratha?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1953162" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13360305286566691010">This was one of the few times where the legendary Famous Five meals took a back seat. However…..a mysterious house called *Smuggler’s Top* perched atop the mist-shrouded Castaway Hill more than adequately made up for the absence of lashings of quintessential Blyton culinary fare.<br/><br/>The adventure starts off with the Five heading off to Kirrin Island for their much-loved, idyllic vacation. But a terrible gale puts a damper in plans when it uproots a massive ash tree straight onto Kirrin Cottage. A ho</span>
  <span id="freeText13360305286566691010" style="display:none">This was one of the few times where the legendary Famous Five meals took a back seat. However…..a mysterious house called *Smuggler’s Top* perched atop the mist-shrouded Castaway Hill more than adequately made up for the absence of lashings of quintessential Blyton culinary fare.<br /><br />The adventure starts off with the Five heading off to Kirrin Island for their much-loved, idyllic vacation. But a terrible gale puts a damper in plans when it uproots a massive ash tree straight onto Kirrin Cottage. A house badly in need of repairs, a guilty but easily irritable Uncle Quentin, four active children and one big, excitable dog…..poor Aunt Fanny is in a right mess. <br /><br />But surprisingly enough, Uncle Quentin comes up with a plan of parcelling the children off to his fellow scientist, Mr. Lenoir’s home. The house goes by the name of Smuggler’s Top and if that wasn’t incentive enough, an added attraction is Sooty (Pierre Lenoir), Sr. Lenoir’s stepson who is a legendary prankster at Dick’s school.<br /><br />Smuggler’s Top is an ancient house steeped in mystery. There are hidden passages behind panels, deep pits under ordinary floors that lead into a honeycomb of passages, some known and others where a clueless person could get lost in…forever.<br />If all this wasn’t exciting enough for the restless Five, they soon discover that someone is flashing signals in the dead of night from one of the towers of Smuggler’s Top. Who could it be and what does it mean? Do the constantly creeping mists hide undiscovered smuggling activities? Is their mercurial host, Mr.Lenoir up to something sinister? And what role does the nasty, supposedly-deaf man-servant Block play in all these happenings? <br /><br />The mystery unfolds with many strange happenings and the Five are plunged into adventure yet again!<br /><br />I enjoyed this book mainly for the atmosphere created by Enid Blyton. It was also filled with many interesting and dubious characters right from the cold Mr.Lenoir to the slightly eccentric smuggler, Mr.Barling. As a child, I kept second-guessing the motives of the supposed anatagonists.<br /><br />Speaking of childhood, I always read “Five Run Away Together” and “Five Go To Smuggler’s Top” back to back. From the windswept beauty of Kirrin Island to the sinister charm of Castaway Hill...each had its own appeal. And while I have replenished my stock of other long-lost Famous Fives…I have loyally preserved that ancient double edition; a slice of my childhood and a reminder of simpler times.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="13360305286566691010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2865015703" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Kaitlin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin"><img alt="Kaitlin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1481663259p2/19126246.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2865015703?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kaitlin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kaitlin" href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin">Kaitlin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2865015703" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13387056351752978372">This one was one of my favourites as a child and I am certain I have read it about a thousand times. I think this one is one of the few where there is more camaraderie, more family involvement with Quentin being in the picture, and more of a mystery and plot too. <br/><br/>This one follows the five as they go to live at smuggler's top whilst the repairs are made on their cottage. They have a friend there who goes to school with the boys, and they all end up trying to track down a smuggler in the town.<br/><br/>4*s</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_727692692" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/727692692" />
    <a title="Bionic Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean"><img alt="Bionic Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586709362p2/19300421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/727692692?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bionic Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bionic Jean" href="/user/show/19300421-bionic-jean">Bionic Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/7047401-five-go-to-smuggler-s-top">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=antiquarian-books">antiquarian-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=read-authors-a-b">read-authors-a-b</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=children-s-1920-1949">children-s-1920-1949</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/19300421-bionic-jean?shelf=children-s-and-ya">children-s-and-ya</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer727692692" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15339933334327644421">I loved the "Famous Five" books when I was a child. Now I have a very different view.<br/><br/>First of all let me say they are great as adventure stories. They describe kids having an active outdoor life - and generally getting mixed up in something exciting. This sounds just like the sort of books we should be encouraging children (who nowadays are potential couch/computer potatoes) to read. And generally I would encourage all types of reading - comics, interactive books, whatever. But since there is su</span>
  <span id="freeText15339933334327644421" style="display:none">I loved the "Famous Five" books when I was a child. Now I have a very different view.<br /><br />First of all let me say they are great as adventure stories. They describe kids having an active outdoor life - and generally getting mixed up in something exciting. This sounds just like the sort of books we should be encouraging children (who nowadays are potential couch/computer potatoes) to read. And generally I would encourage all types of reading - comics, interactive books, whatever. But since there is such an abundance, do we have to select such inappropriate material?<br /><br />I reread a Famous Five book recently and was shocked and appalled. If there's a black face you can guarantee it will be a Bad Man (in this case, a thief. Apparently the message of another book is not to leave anything valuable around if black children are there, because they will steal it. Another? "What a funny little creature!" says one of the boys when he finds a lost child with a brown face in the snow.) What?<br /><br />Google "Enid Blyton racism" and you find lots of articles. Her home town is very divided as some regard her as an icon and others actually remember her as a "nasty piece of work" - a sexist, racist, antisemitic, snob. The sexism is flagrant in her books too. The girls are always "helping mother to cook the dinner" or doing some other little domestic task. Not the boys, of course.<br /><br />All the children are arrogant and patronising to various of the folk they meet, such as a caretaker whom they consider to be beneath their social class. It may be worth mentioning the children's names here. Again, are these likely to click with many modern readers? Julian (a boy) Tommy (a dog) Dick (a boy) George (a girl) and Anne (a girl), I remember myself being rather confused by these names as a child. <br /><br />"Enid", a recent award-winning film about the life of Enid Blyton, showed that her home life was very different from her public image. She was the queen of PR - and very driven. Her children hardly got to see her except in the company of a group of local children when she did public readings. Everyone's favourite auntie - including mine I have to say. I was a proud member of the "Famous Five Club"!<br /><br />Having established that her books have exciting story lines it has to be said that they are very restricted in the vocabulary they use. This is probably partly why kids love them of course! And there's absolutely no harm in reading this type of easy book with lots of repetition for pleasure, as an extra outside school. But there is a plethora of other options - books which are not contentious in this way, where the attitudes shown are far less questionable and socially damaging.<br /><br />Who reads Enid Blyton nowadays? I have a feeling that she is read mostly by adults, in a spirit of nostalgia. I doubt very much whether many of today's children choose to read about the Famous Five. Most will have the discernment to read exactly what they choose. Younger children of course will have their books chosen for them by adults. Some of these picture books may well be by Enid Blyton, who will be fondly remembered by their parents. After all, she wrote dozens of books and there are probably some in there which are free of this damaging potential influence. Your children love her? They are enthralled by her magic? Fine. But what I would say is PLEASE read the book yourself before letting your child read it. If it's inappropriate then discuss why. Put it in its historical context - discuss prejudice of all types, class distinctions etc.<br /><br />Be aware! <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15339933334327644421" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_234873344" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/234873344" />
    <a title="Kirsti" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5581493-kirsti"><img alt="Kirsti" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1497065220p2/5581493.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/234873344?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kirsti" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kirsti" href="/user/show/5581493-kirsti">Kirsti</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer234873344" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13250905852180314273">"Five go to smuggler's top" was actually one of the last Five books I read, even though it is number 4 in the series. It was hard to find a copy originally, and I had to make do with a more modern publication than some of the others I own.<br/><br/>What starts off as a lovely easter holidays at Kirrin soon turns into adventure, with a large tree hitting the house making it impossible for the children to stay there. They are packed off to stay with a friend of Uncle Quentin's, and his son, a boy Julian and</span>
  <span id="freeText13250905852180314273" style="display:none">"Five go to smuggler's top" was actually one of the last Five books I read, even though it is number 4 in the series. It was hard to find a copy originally, and I had to make do with a more modern publication than some of the others I own.<br /><br />What starts off as a lovely easter holidays at Kirrin soon turns into adventure, with a large tree hitting the house making it impossible for the children to stay there. They are packed off to stay with a friend of Uncle Quentin's, and his son, a boy Julian and Dick know from school, Sooty. The house is a mysterious one, filled with secret passageways. It's a good thing too, because Mr Lenoir doesn't like dogs, and the children have to hide Timmy during the day.<br /><br />Smugglers, mystery and intrigue are the order of the day, culminating in the kidnapping of Uncle Quentin and Sooty beneath the catacombs of the house. Lucky Timmy is there to save the day, bite the kidnappers and eventually bring them to justice! He has a close call of his own, almost drowning in the swamp, but Uncle Quentin's quick thinking saves the dog he always seems to dislike.<br /><br />A quick paced, rollicking adventure that is still as much fun as when I picked it up years ago. I do love me some Famous Five! </span>
  <a data-text-id="13250905852180314273" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Kelly-Marie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/57484845-kelly-marie"><img alt="Kelly-Marie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596775233p2/57484845.jpg" /></a>

<span id="freeTextContainer5293821597777973342">Things get off to a cold and windy start in this fourth installment of the Famous Five series, as the four chums (plus Timmy!) arrive in the country for holls only to discover that Kirrin Cottage is not only cold, but battered by wind! "It's awfully cold!" opines sweet Anne, "and windy!" Anne, of course, will make a man a good little housewife some day. "It's the wind," says Aunt Fanny, who is already a good little housewife, "making it cold!" Julian, however, who is a tall, strong boy with tall</span>
  <span id="freeText5293821597777973342" style="display:none">Things get off to a cold and windy start in this fourth installment of the Famous Five series, as the four chums (plus Timmy!) arrive in the country for holls only to discover that Kirrin Cottage is not only cold, but battered by wind! "It's awfully cold!" opines sweet Anne, "and windy!" Anne, of course, will make a man a good little housewife some day. "It's the wind," says Aunt Fanny, who is already a good little housewife, "making it cold!" Julian, however, who is a tall, strong boy with tall, strong boy parts, is rather of the masculine and therefore correct opinion that the cold is somehow stirring the wind! It's a queer thing when a tree immediately falls onto Kirrin Cottage and prevents Julian and Aunt Fanny from coming to meteorological loggerheads. Uncle Quentin quickly motors the four (plus Timmy!) off to Smuggler's Top!<br /><br />Smuggler's Top is a queer house with a lot of queer things happening in it, but luckily the four chums (plus Timmy!) have a simply ripping holls playing hands of Slap-Down Patience while devouring plenty of hot cocoa, steaming cups of delicious creamy coffee, plenty of jammy buns, hot soup, big and crunchy biscuits, some more jolly good soup, ginger buns hot from the oven, thick and delicious slices of heavenly fruit cake, delicious steamed puddings, chocolate cake, and more top-notch jammy buns! Just when they think they can't eat a bite more, they cheekily raid the larder for a scrumptious spread of cakes, tarts, and even more ginger buns! There's plenty of dog-meat for Timmy, except for the part of the book where for a few days he has to eat rats and lick a cavern wall for moisture. Clever Timmy!<br /><br />Oh, and there's something about an evil ecologically-minded duff of a villain who wants a historic marsh left as it is and who foolhardily attempts to prevent Uncle Quentin from draining the marsh and selling off the land to be developed into a soulless subdivision, thus hindering British capitalism. Jolly rotten commie, wot!</span>
  <a data-text-id="5293821597777973342" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wesley Possible" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wesley Possible" href="/user/show/13329381-wesley-possible">Wesley Possible</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer434465832" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11705492213252112485">Four children and their dog have gone to stay with their Uncle’s friend, only to discover mysterious goings on during their stay. A flashing light in the middle of the night leads them on an adventure to pursue a group of smuggler’s in the area, an adventure which deepens with the disappearances of their Uncle and their close friend. With unsuspecting twists, the children investigate the goings on and get to the bottom of this thrilling encounter.<br/><br/>The highlights of this book was the unexpected tw</span>
  <span id="freeText11705492213252112485" style="display:none">Four children and their dog have gone to stay with their Uncle’s friend, only to discover mysterious goings on during their stay. A flashing light in the middle of the night leads them on an adventure to pursue a group of smuggler’s in the area, an adventure which deepens with the disappearances of their Uncle and their close friend. With unsuspecting twists, the children investigate the goings on and get to the bottom of this thrilling encounter.<br /><br />The highlights of this book was the unexpected twists and excitement created by the author throughout the book. The characters created are realistic and keeps the reader in suspense on who is the suspect who is helping the smuggler’s. The character Block was a particular favourite as the relationship with the children creates a tension and suspicion.<br /><br />The sequence of events kept the reader engaged. The intensity also helped towards this, particularly the kidnapping of Uncle Quentin and Sooty created a sense of urgency and danger to the mystery. From a teaching perspective, there are opportunities to learn about other subjects, for example the dangers of quicksand and Smuggler’s. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11705492213252112485" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText483915619542413054" style="display:none">Another trip into my childhood :) and I enjoyed every minute of it. The five are staying in an old house where the discover secret passages and underground tunnels (a must for children 8 years plus). Are there really smugglers at Smugglers Top? Who's behind the signalling, the kidnapping and all the weird goings on?<br /><br /><br />In a town which is cut off from the rest of the world, the Famous five must go on a spooky adventure to solve the mystery of smugglers top.<br /> This book can be used I the classroom for guided reading sessions as well as for individual reading. If nothing else it is a book which will awaken young people's desire for adventure and get them away from the game consoles which tie them into their sofas.<br /><br />I believe this book should be on the bookshelf of every classroom as it is a very good read. In PSHE / Citizenship, this book can be drawn on to teach children about friendship and caring about what goes on in your community. A must read!<br /><br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_3201036779" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3201036779" />
    <a title="Therese" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6570538-therese"><img alt="Therese" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1566735880p2/6570538.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3201036779?book_show_action=true">Feb 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Therese" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Therese" href="/user/show/6570538-therese">Therese</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6570538-therese?shelf=read-in-english">read-in-english</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6570538-therese?shelf=nostalgia">nostalgia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6570538-therese?shelf=read-in-2020">read-in-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3201036779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14586710531648978683">four books into what i thought would be mostly a re-read of this series and i'm realizing that i have no memory of this book. it's definitely possible that i have either only read the first three, or i have read this like once and stuck to mostly re-reading the first three.<br/><br/>anyway, this felt more mysterious than the others (is block deaf or not? how is he in two places at one time?) but that could just be because i don't remember it from when i was a kid.<br/><br/>i can't leave a short comment on this book</span>
  <span id="freeText14586710531648978683" style="display:none">four books into what i thought would be mostly a re-read of this series and i'm realizing that i have no memory of this book. it's definitely possible that i have either only read the first three, or i have read this like once and stuck to mostly re-reading the first three.<br /><br />anyway, this felt more mysterious than the others (is block deaf or not? how is he in two places at one time?) but that could just be because i don't remember it from when i was a kid.<br /><br />i can't leave a short comment on this book without mentioning that it is definitely racist. i think pierre (i refuse to use that terrible nickname) is a good character, and nothing about his personality or actions stood out to me as being stereotyped really, but i just can't get over him being described as having brown skin and then they give him <i>that</i> nickname. yikes. (sidenote - i saw another (very valid) review mentioning that all the people of color in enid blyton books tend to be the bad guys, and as i am not well versed in enid blyton canon (as evident of me possibly only having read the first three books in this series), i can't for sure if that's true or not, but i don't doubt it. that being said, that is not the case in this book. pierre is never portrayed in any kind of villainous way. enid blyton's still racist though.)<br /><br />(read mostly so i can listen to the famous five podcast)</span>
  <a data-text-id="14586710531648978683" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2878485877?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2019</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3685484722872139422">This was my favourite Famous Five as a kid. Interesting to read it as an adult and see some themes I’d never even noticed (coercive control, gaslighting - bad Mr Lenoir) and women seen as feeble. It was written in 1945 which may explain it but Malory Towers was written in 1948 and doesn’t have the same domestic abuse themes. Guess what I’m saying is that if you hate this book, there will be other Blyton ones you like.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_770513353" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/770513353" />
    <a title="^" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5069314"><img alt="^" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299184103p2/5069314.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/770513353?book_show_action=true">Nov 21, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="^" class="user" itemprop="url" name="^" href="/user/show/5069314">^</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/18877695-five-go-to-smuggler-s-top">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=review-liked">review-liked</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=author-female">author-female</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=exploration">exploration</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=books-with-10001-to-100000-ratings">books-with-10001-to-100000-ratings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5069314?shelf=y-1941-1950">y-1941-1950</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer770513353" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12325572054002240202">Smugglers, trusting parents, tunnels, baddies with appropriate names such as ‘Block’… this is what childhood is all about. For an adult this is hardly demanding reading; but it does bring back happy childhood memories of the picnics we ate (an awful lot of hard-boiled eggs … which nowadays might be viewed as a cholesterol risk!) and the games of adventure and tremendous imagination we used to play outdoors after reading books such as those in the Famous Five series. I don’t care how good compute</span>
  <span id="freeText12325572054002240202" style="display:none">Smugglers, trusting parents, tunnels, baddies with appropriate names such as ‘Block’… this is what childhood is all about. For an adult this is hardly demanding reading; but it does bring back happy childhood memories of the picnics we ate (an awful lot of hard-boiled eggs … which nowadays might be viewed as a cholesterol risk!) and the games of adventure and tremendous imagination we used to play outdoors after reading books such as those in the Famous Five series. I don’t care how good computer-generated graphics get; they’ll never possess the imaginative, immersive power of the complex mouldy pong which wreathes around damp castles; or the heart-stopping feeling of slipping feet attempting to climb a rope ladder. Best of all, this edition is historically correct and has not been tampered with (edited) to indoctrinate later generations with wet politically correct sensitivities which didn’t worm their way into urban British society until the 1990s.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12325572054002240202" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Vig2piHCzI/yAfgSVbSBDD20g9YxMOjPSuir/BXFERTkyMe7WlS5zJGACYWWgbYCAbhKSRwhjuuqyfily0YV4Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  <span id="freeText7151404502003324742" style="display:none">See also:<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15266907.______________" title="Ένιντ Μπλάιτον" rel="nofollow noopener">Ένιντ Μπλάιτον</a> (Greek)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16081267.Enida_Blaitona" title="Enida Blaitona" rel="nofollow noopener">Enida Blaitona</a> (Latvian)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7025402.____________" title="Энид Блайтон" rel="nofollow noopener">Энид Блайтон</a> (Russian)<br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/18191379.Inid_Blajton" title="Inid Blajton" rel="nofollow noopener">Inid Blajton</a> (Serbian)<br /><br />Enid Mary Blyton (1897 - 1968) was an English author of children's books.<br /><br />Born in South London, Blyton was the eldest of three children, and showed an early interest in music and reading. She was educated at St. Christopher's School, Beckenham, and - having decided not to pursue her music - at Ipswich High School, where she trained as a kindergarten teacher. She taught for five years before her 1924 marriage to editor Hugh Pollock, with whom she had two daughters. This marriage ended in divorce, and Blyton remarried in 1943, to surgeon Kenneth Fraser Darrell Waters. She died in 1968, one year after her second husband.<br /><br />Blyton was a prolific author of children's books, who penned an estimated 800 books over about 40 years. Her stories were often either children's adventure and mystery stories, or fantasies involving magic. Notable series include: The Famous Five, The Secret Seven, The Five Find-Outers, Noddy, The Wishing Chair, Mallory Towers, and St. Clare's.<br /><br />According to the Index Translationum, Blyton was the fifth most popular author in the world in 2007, coming after Lenin but ahead of Shakespeare.<br /><br /><i>See also her pen name <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/13663105.Mary_Pollock" title="Mary Pollock" rel="nofollow noopener">Mary Pollock</a></i></span>

About The Author: Eni<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText15722278993250174273\" style=\"display:none\">What can Uncle Quentin be up to - all alone - on Kirrin Island. He won\'t let anyone visit - not even George and the rest of the Famous Five. But Quentin isn\'t really all alone on the island - somebody is watching his every move! About The Author: Enid Blyton, 1896 - November 28, 1959 Enid Blyton was born in London in 1896. She was educated in a private school and thought that she would become a musician until she realized that writing was her passion. She attended Ipswich High School where she trained to become a kindergarten teacher and eventually opened her own school for infants. Blyton\'s first poem was published in 1917, entitled &quot;Have You-&quot; which appeared in Nash\'s Magazine. In 1922, her first book of verses was published, entitled &quot;Child Whispers.&quot; In 1926 she accepted a position editing the children\'s magazine &quot;Sunny Stories&quot; as well as writing the column &quot;Teachers World.&quot; Blyton\'s first full length children\'s book was published din 1938 and was titled &quot;The Secret Island.&quot; After working on the column for years, Blyton quit &quot;Teachers World&quot; in 1945 and also ended her stint as editor of &quot;Sunny Stories&quot; seven years later. In 1953 she started her own children\'s magazine called &quot;The Edith Blyton Magazine&quot; which featured stories about her characters and news on the clubs formed around them. Her most famous stories were those of the &quot;Famous Five&quot; The Magazine closed in 1959. In the 50\'s and 60\'s Blyton was criticized for the language in her book, for being to simple, but some 300 are still in print today. Blyton has published over 600 books in the course of her career. Enid Blyton died in her sleep on November 28, 1968. The Famous<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText1182506839623173842\" style=\"display:none\">The Famous Five are having a brilliant time - on holiday in horse-drawn caravans - and they\'ve discovered a ruined castle nearby! The castle looked deserted from a distance - but is that a face at the window? Or is it a trick of the light? The Famous Five just have to find out! Just who is hiding in the castle? About The Author: Enid Blyton, 1896 - November 28, 1959 Enid Blyton was born in London in 1896. She was educated in a private school and thought that she would become a musician until she realized that writing was her passion. She attended Ipswich High School where she trained to become a kindergarten teacher and eventually opened her own school for infants. Blyton\'s first poem was published in 1917, entitled &quot;Have You-&quot; which appeared in Nash\'s Magazine. In 1922, her first book of verses was published, entitled &quot;Child Whispers.&quot; In 1926 she accepted a position editing the children\'s magazine &quot;Sunny Stories&quot; as well as writing the column &quot;Teachers World.&quot; Blyton\'s first full length children\'s book was published din 1938 and was titled &quot;The Secret Island.&quot; After working on the column for years, Blyton quit &quot;Teachers World&quot; in 1945 and also ended her stint as editor of &quot;Sunny Stories&quot; seven years later. In 1953 she started her own children\'s magazine called &quot;The Edith Blyton Magazine&quot; which featured stories about her characters and news on the clubs formed around them. Her most famous stories were those of the &quot;Famous Five&quot; The Magazine closed in 1959. In the 50\'s and 60\'s Blyton was criticized for the language in her book, for being to simple, but some 300 are still in print today. Blyton has published over 600 books in the course of her career. Enid Blyton died in her sleep on November 28, 1968.
